Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of the Exclusive Role of Syllogism for Assent
Critique of the logicians' claim that syllogism is the exclusive source of assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that desired assents are not confined to syllogism and identify which aspect of syllogism he validates.

Core Idea
Syllogism is valid but not the exclusive source of assent; many assents are obtained without formal reasoning.

In the third and fourth stations, Ibn Taymiyya addresses the logicians' parallel claim that all desired assents (tasdiqat) require syllogism. He demolishes the claim of exclusivity while affirming the validity of syllogism as a reasoning tool. This lesson examines his distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim about assent
Locate the claim in the third station: that none of the desired assents are attained except through the logicians' syllogism. This is a universal negative claim.
The Arabic states: 'إن شيئاً من «التصديقات المطلوبة لا ينال إلا بما ذكروه من القياس'
2
Understand Ibn Taymiyya's refutation
He argues that this universal negation is impossible to know and no proof can establish it. Furthermore, many assents are obtained without syllogism, just as conceptions are obtained without definition.
He points to the existence of desired assents obtained through other means as evidence of the claim's falsehood.
3
Recognize his validation of syllogism
In the fourth station, Ibn Taymiyya explicitly states that the fact that a syllogism composed of two premises yields a conclusion is sound in itself ('أمر صحيح في نفسه'). He does not reject syllogism as a method.
This concession is important: he distinguishes between the valid logical tool and the false claim of exclusivity.
4
Distinguish the valid use from the exclusive claim
Ibn Taymiyya accepts syllogism as a valid means of reasoning but denies that it is the only means. Assents can come from direct experience, testimony, and other forms of reasoning.
This preserves the epistemological diversity of Islamic sciences while acknowledging the utility of formal logic.

Ibn Taymiyya on Proof and Variable Premises
Definition of proof and variation in premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's definition of al-dalil and identify how the number of premises in a proof depends on the seeker's prior knowledge.

Core Idea
A proof entails its conclusion; its premises are not fixed but depend on what the seeker already knows.

Ibn Taymiyya opens with a definition of proof (dalil) and immediately challenges the notion that all proofs require a fixed number of premises. He argues that the number of premises varies according to the knowledge of the person seeking the proof. Through the example of proving that nabidh is haram, he shows that only the missing link needs to be supplied.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of al-dalil
Locate the sentence in the Arabic where Ibn Taymiyya defines al-dalil. Note the key phrase: 'الدليل ما كان موصلاً إلى المطلوب'. This is a lexical definition indicating that anything that leads to the sought can be a proof.
2
Distinguish between dalil and amara
Observe that some theologians restrict 'dalil' to what yields certainty and call what yields opinion 'amara'. Ibn Taymiyya attributes this distinction to the Mu'tazila.
This distinction is important in Usul al-Fiqh.
3
Analyze the entailment relation (luzum)
Ibn Taymiyya states that the proof is the necessary condition (malzum) for the proven (lazim). Understand that if A entails B, then A is a proof for B, regardless of whether A and B are positive or negative.
4
Examine the example of nabidh
Read the example: a person knows that khamr is haram and that nabidh is intoxicating, but is unsure whether every intoxicant is khamr. He only needs the hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr' to complete the proof. Note which premises are already known.
This demonstrates how prior knowledge reduces the number of premises needed.
5
Formalize the categorical syllogism
The implicit argument: All intoxicants are khamr (major premise), all khamr is haram (known), nabidh is intoxicant (known). Therefore nabidh is haram. Identify the missing premise that was supplied.
This shows that only one premise needed to be clarified; the rest were already accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اختلاف عدد المقدمات باختلاف علم المستدل

«الدليل» ما كان موصلاً إلى المطلوب

فكذلك الدليل و البرهان فإن الدليل هو المرشد إلى المطلوب والموصل إلى المقصود وكل ما كان مستلزماً لغيره فإنه يمكن أن يستدل به عليه. ولهذا قيل: الدليل ما يكون النظر الصحيح فيه موصلاً إلى علم أو ظن. وبعض المتكلمين يخص لفظ «الدليل» بما يوصل إلى العلم، ويسمى ما يوصل إلى الظن «أمارة». وهذا اصطلاح من اصطلح عليه من المعتزلة ومن تلقاه عنهم .

فالمقصود أن كل ما كان مستلزماً لغيره بحيث يكون ملزوماً له فإنه يكون دليلاً عليه وبرهاناً له، سواءً كانا وجوديين أو عدميين أو أحدهما وجودياً والآخر عدمياً. فأبداً الدليل ملزوم للمدلول عليه، والمدلول لازم للدليل .

ثم قد يكون الدليل مقدمة واحدة متى عُلمتْ عُلم المطلوب. وقد يحتاج المستدل إلى مقدمتين، وقد يحتاج إلى ثلاث مقدمات وأربع وخمس، وأكثر، ليس لذلك حدّ مقدر يتساوى فيه جميعُ الناس في جميع المطالب بل ذلك بحسب علم المستدل الطالب بأحوال المطلوب، والدليل، ولوازم ذلك وملزوماته .

فإذا قدر أنه قد عرف ما به يُعلم المطلوب إلا مقدمة واحدة، كان دليله الذي يحتاج إلى بيانه له تلك المقدمة كمن علم أن الخمر محرّم ، وعلم أن النبيذ المتنازع فيه مسكر ، لكن لم يعلم أن كل مسكر هو خمر، فهو لا يحتاج إلا إلى هذه المقدمة. فإذا قيل : ثبت في الصحيح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: «كل مسكر خمر، حصل مطلوبه ، ولم يحتج إلى أن يقال : كل نبيذ مسكر و كل مسكر خمر، ولا أن يقال: كل مسكر خمر و کل خمر حرام فإن هذا كله معلوم له، لم يكن يخفى عليه إلا أن اسم «الخمر» هل هو مختص ببعض المسكرات كما ظنه طائفة علماء من المسلمين ، أو هو شامل لكل مسكر . فإذا ثبت له عن صاحب الشرع أنه جعله عاماً لا خاصاً حصل مطلوبه. وهذا الحديث في صحيح مسلم، ويروى بلفظين: «كل
The difference in the number of premises according to the knowledge of the seeker of proof.

'The proof (dalil) is that which leads to the sought.'

So also the proof and the demonstration: for the proof is the guide to the sought and the conveyor to the intended. And everything that entails something else can be used as a proof for it. Hence it is said: The proof is that which, when correctly considered, leads to knowledge or opinion. Some theologians restrict the term 'proof' to what leads to knowledge, and call what leads to opinion 'indicator' (amara). This is a convention adopted by the Mu'tazila and those who follow them.

The intended meaning is that everything that entails something else such that it is a necessary condition for it, then it is a proof and demonstration for it, whether both are existential or non-existential, or one is existential and the other non-existential. Thus the proof is always the necessary condition (malzum) for the proven, and the proven is a necessary consequence (lazim) of the proof.

Then the proof might be a single premise, such that when it is known, the sought is known. Or the seeker may need two premises, or three, four, five, or more, without a fixed limit that applies equally to all people in all inquiries; rather, that depends on the knowledge of the seeking person concerning the state of the sought, the proof, and their implications and necessary conditions.

Thus if it is assumed that he knows everything by which the sought is known except one premise, then his proof needs only the clarification of that premise—like one who knows that wine (khamr) is prohibited, and knows that the disputed nabidh is intoxicating, but does not know that every intoxicant is khamr. He needs only this premise. So if it is said: It is established in the Sahih from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Every intoxicant is khamr,' his goal is achieved. He does not need to be told: 'Every nabidh is intoxicant and every intoxicant is khamr,' nor 'Every intoxicant is khamr and every khamr is prohibited,' because all this is known to him. Nothing was hidden from him except whether the word 'khamr' is specific to some intoxicants, as a group of Muslim scholars thought, or is general to all intoxicants. So when it is established from the Lawgiver that He made it general, not specific, his goal is achieved. This hadith is in Sahih Muslim, and it is narrated in two wordings: 'Every...'
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage defines al-dalil (proof) and discusses how the number of premises in a proof varies according to the knowledge of the seeker. Ibn Taymiyya begins by quoting the definition: 'The proof is that which leads to the sought.' He then elaborates that a proof guides and conveys to the desired conclusion. Anything that entails something else can serve as a proof. He distinguishes between proof (dalil) leading to certainty and indicator (amara) leading to opinion, noting that this distinction is a convention of the Mu'tazila. The core principle is that the proof is the necessary condition (malzum) for the proven, and the proven is the necessary consequence (lazim) of the proof, whether they are positive or negative. Next, he addresses the number of premises: it can be one or many, with no fixed number for all people or all inquiries. It depends on what the seeker already knows. He illustrates with a juristic example: a person knows that wine (khamr) is haram and that nabidh is intoxicating, but is uncertain whether every intoxicant is khamr (thus also haram). He only needs one premise—the hadith stating 'Every intoxicant is khamr'—to complete his proof. He does not need additional premises because the others are already known. The example demonstrates that the number of premises in a proof is flexible, not rigid as logicians might claim.

Key Terms
الدليل (al-dalīl)
البرهان (al-burhān)
الأمارة (al-amāra)
المقدمة (al-muqaddima)
اللزوم (al-luzūm)
الملزوم (al-malzūm)
اللازم (al-lāzim)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Ibn Taymiyya's example, why does the person only need one premise (the hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr') to prove that nabidh is haram?
A) Because he already knows that nabidh is intoxicating and that khamr is haram.
B) Because the hadith is from Sahih Muslim and thus authoritative.
C) Because nabidh is explicitly named in the Quran.
D) Because he accepts the opinion of the Mu'tazila.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya teaches that a proof is defined by its entailment of the sought, and the number of its premises is flexible, depending on the knowledge of the seeker.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following fiqh argument: 'Every sale with gharar (excessive uncertainty) is invalid. The sale of a fish in the sea is a sale with gharar. Therefore, it is invalid.' Identify which premise a person might already know and which premise they would need to be taught. Write out the syllogism and state which figure and mood it is.
?
Consider the kalam argument for the existence of God: 'Everything that is temporally originated (hadith) has a cause. The world is temporally originated. Therefore, the world has a cause.' If someone already accepts that the world is temporally originated but doubts the major premise, how many premises does the proof need? Construct a proof with only the necessary premises for such a person.

What Is Mantiq?

The Failure of the Formal Syllogism: Form Without Substance
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the logicians' syllogism as insufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' syllogism (qiyas) fails to guarantee the soundness of a proof.

Core Idea
The syllogism gives only the shape of proof, not the substance of entailment.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya launches a direct critique against the logicians' claim that their syllogism can distinguish correct from incorrect proofs. He argues that the syllogistic form is merely an empty shell, and that true proof relies on something deeper: the actual relation of entailment (luzum) between the evidence and the conclusion. This lesson unpacks his critique and its implications for how we evaluate arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
What do the logicians assert about their syllogism? Look for the claim Ibn Taymiyya refutes: that it distinguishes correct from corrupt proofs.
2
Find Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He says the syllogism only provides the form (sura) and shape (shakl) of the proof. What does he mean by that?
3
Locate the missing element
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is missing from the formal syllogism? It is the verification of whether the proof actually entails its implication (luzum).
4
Consider the role of premises
Ibn Taymiyya notes that the truth of premises is outside the syllogism's domain. Why does this limitation matter for the soundness of an argument?

قياسهم . ولا يقال : إن قياسهم يعرف صحيحَ الأدلة من فاسدها فإن هذا إنما يقوله جاهل لا يعرف حقيقة قياسهم. فإن قياسهم ليس فيه إلا شكل الدليل وصورته. وأما كون الدليل المعين مستلزماً لمدلوله فهذا ليس في قياسهم ما يتعرض له بنفي ولا إثبات وإنما هذا علمه بالمقدمات التي اشتمل عليها الدليل، وليس في قياسهم بيان صحة شيء بحسب المقدمات ولا فسادها وإنما يتكلمون في هذا إذا تكلموا في مواد القياس، وهو من الكلام فمن عرف أن هذا لازم لهذا استدل بالملزوم على اللازم، وإن لم يذكر لفظ «اللزوم» ولا تَصَوَّر معنى هذا اللفظ . بل من عرف أن كذا لا بد له من كذا ، أو أنه إذا كان كذا كان كذا، وأمثال هذا، فقد علم اللزوم». كما يعرف أن كل ما في الوجود فهو آية الله ، فإنه مفتقر إليه محتاج إليه، لا بد له منه فيلزم من وجوده وجود الصانع . وكما يعلم أن المحدث لا بد له من محدث، كما قال تعالى : ﴿ أَمْ خُلِقُوا مِنْ غَيْرِ شَيْءٍ أَمْ هُمُ الْخَلِقُونَ ﴾ [سورة الطور : ٣٥]. وفي الصحيحين عن جبير بن مُطْعِم أنه لما قدم في فداء الأسرى عام بدر سمع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يقرأ في المغرب بـ «الطور». قال: «فلما سمعت قوله : أَمْ خُلِقُوا مِنْ غَيْرِ شَيْءٍ أَمْ هُمُ الْخَلِقُونَ ﴾ [سورة الطور : ٣٥] أحسستُ بفؤادي قد انصدع . فإن هذا تقسيم حاصر . يقول : أخُلِقوا من غير خالقِ خَلَقهم؟ فهذا ممتنع في بداية وجه الدليل في آية الطور العقول . أم هم خَلَقوا أنفسهم؟ فهذا أشدّ امتناعاً. فعلم أن لهم خالقاً خَلقهم. وهو سبحانه ذكر الدليل بصيغة استفهام الإنكار ليبيّن أن هذه القضية التي استدل بها فطرية، بديهية، مستقرة في النفوس، لا يمكن أحداً إنكارها . فلا يمكن صحيح الفطرة أن يدعي وجود حادث بدون محدث أحدثه، ولا يمكنه أن يقول هو أحدث نفسه . وقد بسطنا الكلام على ما قاله الناس في هذا المقام من الحدوث والإمكان علة الافتقار إلى There is nothing in their syllogism except the form of the proof without showing its correctness or corruption. What is intended here is that what is sought is knowledge, and the way to it is the proof. So whoever knows the proof of his sought object knows his sought object, whether he organizes it in their syllogism or not. And whoever does not know his proof, their syllogism does not benefit him. And it cannot be said that their syllogism distinguishes correct proofs from corrupt ones, for this is only said by an ignorant person who does not know the reality of their syllogism. For their syllogism contains only the shape and form of the proof. As for the fact that a particular proof entails its implication, their syllogism does not address this by negation or affirmation. Rather, this knowledge is of the premises contained in the proof, and their syllogism does not show the correctness of anything according to the premises nor its corruption. They only speak about this when they speak about the matter of the syllogism, which is speech about the premises in terms of what makes them true, and their speech about this contains much error, as was pointed out elsewhere. The real essence considered in every proof is 'entailment' (al-luzum). What is intended here is that the real essence considered in every demonstration and proof in the world is 'entailment'. So whoever knows that this necessarily follows from that uses what necessitates to infer what is necessitated, even if he does not mention the word 'entailment' nor conceptualize the meaning of this word. Rather, whoever knows that such-and-such cannot be without such-and-such, or that if it is such then it is such, and the like, has known entailment. Just as one knows that everything in existence is a sign of Allah, for it is dependent upon Him, in need of Him, cannot be without Him, so from its existence the existence of the Creator necessarily follows. And just as one knows that an originated thing must have an originator, as Allah the Exalted said: "Or were they created of nothing, or are they the creators?" [Surah at-Tur: 35]. And in the two Sahihs from Jubayr ibn Mut'im that when he came for the ransom of captives in the year of Badr, he heard the Prophet, peace be upon him, reciting in Maghrib "at-Tur". He said: "When I heard his saying: 'Or were they created of nothing, or are they the creators?' [Surah at-Tur: 35] I felt my heart split." For this is an exhaustive division. He says: Were they created without a creator who created them? This is impossible in the beginning of the minds. Or did they create themselves? This is even more impossible. So they knew that they have a Creator who created them. And He, glorified, mentioned the proof in the form of an interrogative of denial to show that this proposition that he used as proof is innate, self-evident, firmly established in souls, and no one can deny it. So a person of sound innate disposition cannot claim the existence of an originated thing without an originator that originated it, nor can he say that it originated itself. And we have expanded the discussion on what people have said in this context, that temporality and contingency are the cause of need for a cause, and we have mentioned the general ways of the people of the earth in proving the Creator, from the theologians and philosophers, and the ways of the prophets, peace be upon them, and what the general investigators of Islam followed, from the Mu'tazili, Karrami, Kulabi, Ash'ari, philosopher, and others, in more than one place, such as the book 'Dar' Ta'arud al-'Aql wa al-Naql'.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that their syllogistic form is sufficient for distinguishing correct proofs from incorrect ones. He begins by stating that the logicians' syllogism contains only the outward form (sura) of a proof, not the verification of its soundness or corruption. The true aim is knowledge (al-'ilm), and the path to it is the proof (al-dalil). A person who knows the proof of what he seeks will know his sought object regardless of whether he employs the logicians' syllogism. Conversely, if one does not know his proof, the syllogism is useless. Ibn Taymiyya then refutes the claim that the syllogism can distinguish correct from corrupt proofs, calling such a claim ignorance of the reality of their syllogism. The syllogism only provides the shape and image of the proof; the crucial aspect—whether a particular proof actually entails its conclusion (madlul)—is not addressed by their syllogistic form at all. This knowledge comes from understanding the premises (muqaddimat). The logicians only enter into this when discussing the 'matter' (mawadd) of the syllogism, i.e., the truth of the premises, and in that area they make many errors. After this critique, Ibn Taymiyya presents his positive thesis: the real essence (al-haqiqa al-mu'tabara) in every proof is 'luzum' (entailment or necessary connection). Whoever knows that A entails B can use A as evidence for B, even if he lacks the technical term 'luzum'. This is exemplified by the innate recognition that everything in existence is dependent on Allah, so the existence of the universe necessitates the existence of the Creator. Similarly, the fact that an originated thing (hadith) requires an originator (muhdith) is a self-evident truth. He supports this with the Qur'anic verse from Surah at-Tur: 35, which presents an exhaustive division (taqsim hasir): either humans were created from nothing, or they created themselves. Both are impossible, so they must have a Creator. The Prophet's companion Jubayr ibn Mut'im, who was still a polytheist at the time, felt his heart split upon hearing this verse, indicating its powerful, innate persuasiveness. Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that Allah presented this argument in the form of an interrogative of denial (istifham al-inkar) to highlight that the proposition is innate (fitri), self-evident (badihi), and firmly fixed in souls—denying it is impossible for a person of sound disposition. The passage concludes by noting that Ibn Taymiyya has elaborated elsewhere on the various methods of proving the Creator, including the arguments of different theological schools and philosophers, referring to his work 'Dar' Ta'arud al-'Aql wa al-Naql'.

Key Terms
اللزوم
القياس
المقدمات

B) The entailment between proof and conclusion
C) The terms of the syllogism
D) The figure of the syllogism

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the formal syllogism is insufficient for verifying proofs because it does not address the essential relation of entailment (luzum) between evidence and conclusion.

The True Criterion: Entailment (Luzum) and the Qur'anic Proof
Ibn Taymiyya explains luzum and illustrates it with the Qur'anic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of al-luzum and reconstruct the Qur'anic argument for the Creator from Surah at-Tur as an example of innate entailment.

Core Idea
Entailment (luzum) is the real essence of all proof, and the Qur'an prescribes it innately.

After critiquing the formal syllogism, Ibn Taymiyya presents his positive thesis: the true essence of any proof is the relation of necessary entailment (luzum). He illustrates this with the Qur'anic argument for the existence of the Creator, which is based on an exhaustive division that any sound mind immediately recognizes. In this lesson, we explore this positive doctrine and its foundational role in Islamic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of luzum
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the real essence of any proof? It is the entailment (luzum) between the evidence (dalil) and its implication (madlul).
2
Recognize its universality
Ibn Taymiyya says that even someone who doesn't know the technical term can use luzum. How does he illustrate this with the knowledge of the Creator?
3
Analyze the Qur'anic verse
Read Surah at-Tur:35. What two alternatives are negated? Why does this exhaust the possibilities?
The verse asks: 'Or were they created of nothing, or are they the creators?' The third possibility—that they have a Creator—is implied.
4
Reconstruct the argument
The verse implies: If humans were created, they either have a Creator, or they were created from nothing, or they created themselves. The first two are impossible, so a Creator exists. How does this rely on luzum?
The entailment is that the existence of originated beings necessitates the existence of a Creator.
5
Note the innate (fitri) nature
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that this argument is self-evident. How does the story of Jubayr ibn Mut'im support this?
Jubayr, even as a polytheist, felt his heart split upon hearing the verse, indicating its innate persuasiveness.

في المقدمات من جهة ما يصدق بها وكلامهم في هذا فيه خطأ كثير، كما نبه عليه في موضع آخر . الحقيقة المعتبرة في كل دليل هو «اللزوم» والمقصود هنا أن الحقيقة المعتبرة في كل برهان ودليل في العالم هو «اللزوم». فمن عرف أن هذا لازم لهذا استدل بالملزوم على اللازم، وإن لم يذكر لفظ «اللزوم» ولا تَصَوَّر معنى هذا اللفظ . بل من عرف أن كذا لا بد له من كذا ، أو أنه إذا كان كذا كان كذا، وأمثال هذا، فقد علم اللزوم». كما يعرف أن كل ما في الوجود فهو آية الله ، فإنه مفتقر إليه محتاج إليه، لا بد له منه فيلزم من وجوده وجود الصانع . وكما يعلم أن المحدث لا بد له من محدث، كما قال تعالى : ﴿ أَمْ خُلِقُوا مِنْ غَيْرِ شَيْءٍ أَمْ هُمُ الْخَلِقُونَ ﴾ [سورة الطور : ٣٥]. وفي الصحيحين عن جبير بن مُطْعِم أنه لما قدم في فداء الأسرى عام بدر سمع النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يقرأ في المغرب بـ «الطور». قال: «فلما سمعت قوله : أَمْ خُلِقُوا مِنْ غَيْرِ شَيْءٍ أَمْ هُمُ الْخَلِقُونَ ﴾ [سورة الطور : ٣٥] أحسستُ بفؤادي قد انصدع . فإن هذا تقسيم حاصر . يقول : أخُلِقوا من غير خالقِ خَلَقهم؟ فهذا ممتنع في بداية وجه الدليل في آية الطور العقول . أم هم خَلَقوا أنفسهم؟ فهذا أشدّ امتناعاً. فعلم أن لهم خالقاً خَلقهم. وهو سبحانه ذكر الدليل بصيغة استفهام الإنكار ليبيّن أن هذه القضية التي استدل بها فطرية، بديهية، مستقرة في النفوس، لا يمكن أحداً إنكارها . فلا يمكن صحيح الفطرة أن يدعي وجود حادث بدون محدث أحدثه، ولا يمكنه أن يقول هو أحدث نفسه . وقد بسطنا الكلام على ما قاله الناس في هذا المقام من الحدوث والإمكان علة الافتقار إلى المؤثر»، وذكرنا عامة طرائق أهل الأرض في إثبات الصانع من المتكلمين والفلاسفة، وطرق الأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم، وما سلكه عامة نظار الإسلام من معتزليّ، وكَرَّامي، وكُلابي، وأشعري، وفيلسوف، وغيرهم، في غير موضع، مثل کتاب درء تعارض العقل والنقل
There is nothing in their syllogism except the form of the proof without showing its correctness or corruption. What is intended here is that what is sought is knowledge, and the way to it is the proof. So whoever knows the proof of his sought object knows his sought object, whether he organizes it in their syllogism or not. And whoever does not know his proof, their syllogism does not benefit him. And it cannot be said that their syllogism distinguishes correct proofs from corrupt ones, for this is only said by an ignorant person who does not know the reality of their syllogism. For their syllogism contains only the shape and form of the proof. As for the fact that a particular proof entails its implication, their syllogism does not address this by negation or affirmation. Rather, this knowledge is of the premises contained in the proof, and their syllogism does not show the correctness of anything according to the premises nor its corruption. They only speak about this when they speak about the matter of the syllogism, which is speech about the premises in terms of what makes them true, and their speech about this contains much error, as was pointed out elsewhere. The real essence considered in every proof is 'entailment' (al-luzum). What is intended here is that the real essence considered in every demonstration and proof in the world is 'entailment'. So whoever knows that this necessarily follows from that uses what necessitates to infer what is necessitated, even if he does not mention the word 'entailment' nor conceptualize the meaning of this word. Rather, whoever knows that such-and-such cannot be without such-and-such, or that if it is such then it is such, and the like, has known entailment. Just as one knows that everything in existence is a sign of Allah, for it is dependent upon Him, in need of Him, cannot be without Him, so from its existence the existence of the Creator necessarily follows. And just as one knows that an originated thing must have an originator, as Allah the Exalted said: "Or were they created of nothing, or are they the creators?" [Surah at-Tur: 35]. And in the two Sahihs from Jubayr ibn Mut'im that when he came for the ransom of captives in the year of Badr, he heard the Prophet, peace be upon him, reciting in Maghrib "at-Tur". He said: "When I heard his saying: 'Or were they created of nothing, or are they the creators?' [Surah at-Tur: 35] I felt my heart split." For this is an exhaustive division. He says: Were they created without a creator who created them? This is impossible in the beginning of the minds. Or did they create themselves? This is even more impossible. So they knew that they have a Creator who created them. And He, glorified, mentioned the proof in the form of an interrogative of denial to show that this proposition that he used as proof is innate, self-evident, firmly established in souls, and no one can deny it. So a person of sound innate disposition cannot claim the existence of an originated thing without an originator that originated it, nor can he say that it originated itself. And we have expanded the discussion on what people have said in this context, that temporality and contingency are the cause of need for a cause, and we have mentioned the general ways of the people of the earth in proving the Creator, from the theologians and philosophers, and the ways of the prophets, peace be upon them, and what the general investigators of Islam followed, from the Mu'tazili, Karrami, Kulabi, Ash'ari, philosopher, and others, in more than one place, such as the book 'Dar' Ta'arud al-'Aql wa al-Naql'.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the logicians' claim that their syllogistic form is sufficient for distinguishing correct proofs from incorrect ones. He begins by stating that the logicians' syllogism contains only the outward form (sura) of a proof, not the verification of its soundness or corruption. The true aim is knowledge (al-'ilm), and the path to it is the proof (al-dalil). A person who knows the proof of what he seeks will know his sought object regardless of whether he employs the logicians' syllogism. Conversely, if one does not know his proof, the syllogism is useless. Ibn Taymiyya then refutes the claim that the syllogism can distinguish correct from corrupt proofs, calling such a claim ignorance of the reality of their syllogism. The syllogism only provides the shape and image of the proof; the crucial aspect—whether a particular proof actually entails its conclusion (madlul)—is not addressed by their syllogistic form at all. This knowledge comes from understanding the premises (muqaddimat). The logicians only enter into this when discussing the 'matter' (mawadd) of the syllogism, i.e., the truth of the premises, and in that area they make many errors. After this critique, Ibn Taymiyya presents his positive thesis: the real essence (al-haqiqa al-mu'tabara) in every proof is 'luzum' (entailment or necessary connection). Whoever knows that A entails B can use A as evidence for B, even if he lacks the technical term 'luzum'. This is exemplified by the innate recognition that everything in existence is dependent on Allah, so the existence of the universe necessitates the existence of the Creator. Similarly, the fact that an originated thing (hadith) requires an originator (muhdith) is a self-evident truth. He supports this with the Qur'anic verse from Surah at-Tur: 35, which presents an exhaustive division (taqsim hasir): either humans were created from nothing, or they created themselves. Both are impossible, so they must have a Creator. The Prophet's companion Jubayr ibn Mut'im, who was still a polytheist at the time, felt his heart split upon hearing this verse, indicating its powerful, innate persuasiveness. Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that Allah presented this argument in the form of an interrogative of denial (istifham al-inkar) to highlight that the proposition is innate (fitri), self-evident (badihi), and firmly fixed in souls—denying it is impossible for a person of sound disposition. The passage concludes by noting that Ibn Taymiyya has elaborated elsewhere on the various methods of proving the Creator, including the arguments of different theological schools and philosophers, referring to his work 'Dar' Ta'arud al-'Aql wa al-Naql'.

Key Terms
اللزوم
التقسيم الحاصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is the real essence (haqiqa mu'tabara) of every proof?
A) The syllogistic form
B) The truth of the premises
C) The entailment (luzum) between evidence and conclusion
D) The linguistic phrasing

Key Takeaway
The true essence of any proof is the necessary entailment (luzum) between the evidence and what it proves, a relation that is innate in sound human nature and exemplified by the Qur'anic argument for the Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using Ibn Taymiyya's concept of luzum, explain why the argument 'The world is contingent, therefore it has a Creator' is valid even without formal syllogistic structure.
?
Find another Qur'anic verse that presents an implicit entailment argument (e.g., the argument from design in Surah al-Ghashiyah). Explain how luzum operates there.

Sensory Knowledge vs. Astronomical Calculation: Ibn Taymiyya on True Dawn
Ibn Taymiyya defines true dawn and asserts it is known by sense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's argument that true dawn is known by sense, not by astronomical calculation, and identify the epistemological implications.

Core Idea
Knowledge of dawn comes from the eye, not the clock.

Ibn Taymiyya rebukes astronomers who try to quantify dawn by celestial motion. He insists that true dawn is a sensory phenomenon—sunlight reflected from the atmosphere—and therefore must be observed, not calculated. This lesson unpacks his definition and critique.

Guided Path
1
Extract the definition of true dawn
Find in the Arabic the phrase defining dawn. Note that it is the continuous sunlight (نور الشمس المتصل) that does not cease, unlike the first dawn after which darkness follows.
2
Identify the primary mode of knowing
Ibn Taymiyya states it is known by sensory perception (بالحس والمشاهدة), just like the crescent moon. This is the direct, empirical method.
This mirrors the method for sighting the moon in hadith.
3
Identify the secondary mode of knowing
He mentions an approximate analogy (قياس تقريبي) based on star positions at dawn, but this is only an approximation and secondary to direct observation.
This qiyas is not the logical syllogism but a practical inference.
4
Analyze the criticism of astronomers
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly calls those who fix a portion of dawn equal to twilight as mistaken. The reason is that dawn is reflected light from the atmosphere, not a fixed celestial motion.
He compares them to those who fix an absolute arc of vision, another error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع ـ العلم بطلوع الفجر الفجر هو نور الشمس المتصل الذي لا ينقطع، بخلاف الفجر الأول فإنه تأتي بعده ظلمة . والاعتبار في الشرع في الصلاة والصيام وغير ذلك بالثاني. وهو يعرف بالحس والمشاهدة، كما يُعرف الهلال. ويعرف بالقياس على ما قَرُب منه تقريباً إذا عرف عند انعكاس طلوعه مواضع الكواكب من السماء، فيستدل في اليوم الثاني بذلك على وقت طلوعه وأما تقدير حصة الفجر بأمر محدود من حركة الفلك مساو لحصة العشاء كما فعله سبب ظهور طائفةٌ من الموقتين فغلطوا في ذلك، كما غَلِط من قدَّر قوس الرؤية تقديراً مطلقاً. وذلك لأن الفجر نور الشمس، وهو شعاعها المنعكس الذي يكون من الهواء والأرض.
The Fourth Station: Knowledge of the rising of dawn. Dawn is the continuous light of the sun that does not cease, unlike the first dawn, after which comes darkness. The consideration in the Sacred Law regarding prayer, fasting, and other matters is for the second [dawn]. It is known by sense and observation, just as the crescent moon is known. It can be known by approximate analogy to what is close to it when the positions of the celestial bodies in the sky are known at its rising, so on the second day one infers its time of rising. As for estimating the portion of dawn with a limited matter from the motion of the celestial sphere equal to the portion of twilight, as some astronomers have done, they are mistaken in that, just as those who estimated the arc of vision absolutely have erred. That is because dawn is the light of the sun, which is its reflected rays that come from the air and earth.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage defines the true dawn (al-fajr al-thani) as the continuous sunlight that appears and remains, unlike the false dawn (al-fajr al-awwal) which is followed by darkness. Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that in Islamic law, the second dawn is the one considered for prayer, fasting, etc. He then explains that knowledge of dawn can be obtained through direct sensory observation (hiss wa mushahada) like the crescent moon, or through approximate analogy (qiyas) when one knows the positions of stars. He criticizes astronomers who try to estimate the duration of dawn based on a fixed motion of the celestial sphere equal to twilight, calling this a mistake similar to those who fix an absolute arc of vision. The reason is that dawn is sunlight reflected from the atmosphere and earth, not a purely celestial phenomenon. The commentary should note that Ibn Taymiyya is defending empirical, sensory knowledge over speculative astronomical calculations. All examples are from Islamic religious practice (prayer, fasting) and natural observation.

Key Terms
الفجر
الحس
القياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, how is the true dawn primarily known?
A) By astronomical calculation based on celestial motion
B) By direct sensory observation
C) By analogy with twilight
D) By divine revelation alone

Key Takeaway
True dawn is a sensory phenomenon, not a calculable abstraction; its knowledge depends on direct observation, just as the crescent moon is known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: You are a mufti. Someone asks: 'Is it valid to pray Fajr based solely on an astronomical timetable?' Using Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning, construct a response that explains the role of sensory knowledge.
?
Task 2: Compare Ibn Taymiyya's definition of fajr with the definition given by a modern astronomer (e.g., sun at 18 degrees below horizon). Identify where they differ in genus or differentia.

What Is Mantiq?

Classification of Sabians into Two Types
Author classifies Sabians into monotheists and polytheists with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two types of Sabians as defined by Ibn Taymiyya and identify the Quranic evidence for each.

Core Idea
Sabians are not a single monolith — Ibn Taymiyya splits them into monotheists and polytheists using Quranic verses.

Ibn Taymiyya's classification of the Sabians is a case study in how careful definitional distinctions are essential in Islamic theology. Many assume all Sabians are polytheists, but Ibn Taymiyya shows that the Quran itself distinguishes between monotheist Sabians who are praised and polytheist ones. In this lesson, you will learn to identify these two types and the scriptural basis for each.

Guided Path
1
Extract the two categories
Find the sentence 'الصابئة نوعان : صابئة حُنَفاء موحدون وصابئة مشركون'. This is the core division: monotheist Sabians (Hanafa') and polytheist Sabians.
2
Find the Quranic proof for monotheist type
Locate verse 2:62 (إِنَّ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَالَّذِينَ هَادُوا وَالنَّصَارَىٰ وَالصَّابِئِينَ...). Note that Sabians are listed among those who can be saved if they believe in Allah and the Last Day and do righteous deeds.
This verse is used to support the existence of monotheist Sabians.
3
Find the Quranic proof for polytheist type
Locate verse 22:17 (إِنَّ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَالَّذِينَ هَادُوا وَالصَّابِئِينَ وَالنَّصَارَىٰ وَالْمَجُوسَ وَالَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا...). Here Sabians are listed with Magians and polytheists, and there is no mention of believers among them.
This verse shows that polytheist Sabians exist and are not praised.
4
Understand the historical extension
Ibn Taymiyya explains that monotheist Sabians are followers of Abraham before the Torah and Gospel, while polytheist Sabians later invented polytheism. He also connects polytheist philosophers like Aristotle to the polytheist Sabians.
This step shows how a definition can be expanded by historical context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصابئة نوعان : صابئة حُنَفاء موحدون وصابئة مشركون. فالأولون هم الذين أثنى الله عليهم بقوله تعالى: {إِنَّ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا وَالَّذِينَ هَادُوا وَالنَّصَرَىٰ وَالصَّابِئِينَ مَنْ آمَنَ بِاللَّهِ وَالْيَوْمِ الْآخِرِ وَعَمِلَ صَالِحًا فَلَهُمْ أَجْرُهُمْ عِندَ رَبِّهِمْ وَلَا خَوْفٌ عَلَيْهِمْ وَلَا هُمْ يَحْزَنُونَ} . فأثنى على من آمن بالله واليوم الآخر وعمل صالحاً من هذه الملل الأربع المؤمنين واليهود والنصارى، والصابئين. فهؤلاء كانوا يدينون بالتوراة قبل النسخ والتبديل. وكذلك الذين دانوا بالإنجيل قبل النسخ والتبديل والصابئون الذين كانوا قبل هؤلاء كالمتبعين لملة إبراهيم الحُنفاء - صلى الله عليه وصلى الله على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما صلى على إبراهيم وعلى آل إبراهيم، إنه حميد مجيد - قبل نزول التوراة والإنجيل. وهذا بخلاف المجوس والمشركين، فإنه ليس فيهم مؤمن. فلهذا قال الله تعالى: {إِنَّ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا وَالَّذِينَ هَادُوا وَالصَّابِئِينَ وَالنَّصَرَىٰ وَالْمَجُوسَ وَالَّذِينَ أَشْرَكُوا إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَفْصِلُ بَيْنَهُمْ يَوْمَ الْقِيَامَةِ إِنَّ اللَّهَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ شَهِيدٌ} . فذكر الملل الستّ هؤلاء، وأخبر أنه يفصل بينهم يوم القيامة. لم يذكر في الستُ من كان مؤمناً، وإنما ذكر ذلك في الأربعة فقط. ثم إن الصابئين ابتدعوا الشرك فصاروا مشركين والفلاسفة المشركون من هؤلاء المشركين. وأما قدماء الفلاسفة الذين كانوا يعبدون الله وحده لا يشركون به شيئاً، ويؤمنون بأن الله مُحدث لهذا العالم ويُقرون بمعاد الأبدان، فأولئك من الصابئة الحنفاء الذين أثنى الله عليهم. ثم المشركون من الصابئة كانوا يقرّون بحدوث هذا العالم، كما كان المشركون بقدم العالم من العرب تُقرّ بحدوثه، وكذلك المشركون من الهند. وقد ذكر أهل المقالات أن أول من ظهر عنه القول بقدمه من هؤلاء الفلاسفة المشركين هو أرسطو.
The Sabians are two types: monotheist Sabians (hanafa') and polytheist Sabians. The former are those whom God praised in His saying: 'Indeed, those who have believed and those who were Jews, Christians, and Sabians, whoever believes in God and the Last Day and does righteousness, they shall have their reward with their Lord, and no fear shall come upon them, nor shall they grieve.' So He praised whoever believed in God and the Last Day and did righteous deeds from among these four communities: the believers, Jews, Christians, and Sabians. These used to follow the Torah before its abrogation and alteration. Likewise, those who followed the Gospel before its abrogation and alteration, and the Sabians who were before these, like the followers of the religion of Abraham the hanif — peace be upon him and upon Muhammad and upon the family of Muhammad as You sent blessings upon Abraham and the family of Abraham, indeed You are Praiseworthy, Glorious — before the revelation of the Torah and the Gospel. This is contrary to the Magians and polytheists, for among them there is no believer. Therefore God said: 'Indeed, those who have believed and those who were Jews and the Sabians and Christians and Magians and those who associate partners with God — indeed God will judge between them on the Day of Resurrection. Indeed God is over all things a Witness.' So He mentioned these six communities and informed that He will judge between them on the Day of Resurrection. He did not mention in the six anyone who was a believer; rather He mentioned that in the four only. Then the Sabians invented polytheism and became polytheists, and the polytheist philosophers are among these polytheists. As for the ancient philosophers who used to worship God alone, not associating anything with Him, and believed that God originated this world and affirmed the return of bodies, those are from the monotheist Sabians whom God praised. Then the polytheists among the Sabians used to affirm the temporal origination of this world, just as the polytheists among the Arabs who affirmed its pre-eternity also affirmed its temporal origination, and likewise the polytheists of India. And the authors of articles have mentioned that the first from whom the statement of its pre-eternity appeared among these polytheist philosophers was Aristotle.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the Sabians into two distinct types: monotheist Sabians (Hanafa') and polytheist Sabians. The author begins by stating this twofold division. He then supports the first category with a Quranic verse (2:62) where God praises those who believe in Him and the Last Day and do righteous deeds among Jews, Christians, Sabians, and believers. These Sabians, he explains, followed the Torah before its alteration, and similarly followed the Gospel before alteration, or were followers of Abraham's monotheistic religion before those scriptures. The second category, polytheist Sabians, is contrasted with Magians and polytheists, citing another verse (22:17) that lists six communities but does not specifically mention believers among them. The author then notes that over time, Sabians invented polytheism, and polytheist philosophers emerged from them. However, ancient philosophers who were monotheists and affirmed God's creation of the world and bodily resurrection are counted among the monotheist Sabians. Finally, he reports that the first to explicitly claim the pre-eternity of the world was Aristotle, indicating a corruption among later philosophers.

Key Terms
صابئة (Ṣābi'a): A religious group divided into two types: monotheists (Hanafa') and polytheists; some were followers of Abrahamic monotheism before Torah/Gospel. [Note: In earlier Islamic texts, Sabians often refer to a single group; here Ibn Taymiyya explicitly splits them.]
حنفاء (ḥunafā'): Monotheists inclining to the pure religion of Abraham, opposed to polytheism. [Note: Standard term for monotheists before Islam; stable.]
مشركون (mushrikūn): Polytheists who associate partners with God; includes a faction of Sabians and philosophers. [Note: Stable across tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, how many types of Sabians are there, and which type is praised in the Quran?
A) One type, all praised.
B) Two types: monotheist Sabians are praised, polytheist Sabians are not.
C) Three types: monotheist, polytheist, and philosopher.
D) Two types: polytheist Sabians are praised, monotheist are not.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya divides Sabians into two types — monotheist (Hanafa') and polytheist — each supported by different Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
A group claims to follow Abraham but also worships stars. According to Ibn Taymiyya, are they monotheist or polytheist Sabians? Cite the Quranic evidence from this lesson to support your answer.
?
Using the method of definitional classification, identify the genus and differentia for the term 'Magians' as they appear in the passage (implied contrast).

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
6 min
Dispute over including Necessary Being in substance category
Author highlights dispute over classifying Necessary Being as substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the necessary/possible distinction as a departure from Aristotelian and Islamic theological categories.

Core Idea
The necessary/possible distinction is an Avicennian innovation, not Aristotelian or Islamic.

This passage highlights Ibn Taymiyya's historical critique of how Muslim philosophers like Ibn Sina reclassified the categories of being. He argues that they replaced the sound theological division (qadim/hadith) with a philosophical one (wajib/mumkin) that distorts both Aristotle and Islamic doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debated question
The passage asks whether the Necessary Being falls under the category of substance. Note that the author mentions Aristotle and the ancients did not place it there, and Ibn Sina also refrained.
2
Contrast Aristotle's terminology with Ibn Sina's
Aristotle spoke of 'First Cause' and 'Principle', not 'Necessary Being'. Recognize that the term 'wajib al-wujud' is an Avicennian innovation.
3
Analyze the Aristotelian division of beings
For Aristotle, 'possible' beings that accept existence and non-existence are only originated (hadith), not eternal. The later division into necessary and possible (with possible being eternal) is a departure.
4
Follow Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the innovation
He claims the later philosophers (like Ibn Sina) replaced the theological division (qadim/hadith) with a new one, combining elements from theologians and pagan predecessors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهم منازعون في واجب الوجود هل هو داخل في مقولة «الجوهر»؟ فأرسطو والقدماء ما كانوا يجعلونه من مقولة «الجوهر»، وابن سينا امتنع من ذلك. لكن أرسطو وأتباعه لم يكونوا يقولون : واجب الوجود إنما يقولون العلة الأولى و «المبدأ». وليس في كلام أرسطو تقسيم الموجودات إلى واجب بنفسه و «ممكن بنفسه مع كونه قديماً أزلياً، بل كان الممكن عندهم الذي يقبل الوجود والعدم لا يكون إلا «محدثاً». وإنما قسمه هذه القسمة متأخروهم من الملاحدة الذين انتسبوا إلى الإسلام، كابن سينا وأمثاله، وجعلوا هذا عوضاً عن تقسيم المتكلمين الموجود إلى «قديم» و «حادث»، وسلكوا طريقة وركبوها من كلام المتكلمين ومن كلام سَلَفهم، مثل استدلال أولئك
And they are in dispute concerning the Necessary Being: whether it falls under the category of 'substance'? For Aristotle and the ancients did not make it part of the category of 'substance', and Ibn Sina refrained from that. But Aristotle and his followers did not say 'Necessary Being'—they only say 'the First Cause' and 'the Principle'. And in Aristotle's speech there is no division of existents into 'necessary by itself' and 'possible by itself' with it being eternal and pre-eternal. Rather, the possible in their view—that which accepts existence and non-existence—is only 'originated'. But this division [into necessary and possible] was made by their later [philosophers] from among the atheists who attached themselves to Islam, like Ibn Sina and his likes. And they made this a substitute for the division of the theologians of 'existent' into 'eternal' and 'originated'. And they followed a method combining the speech of the theologians and the speech of their predecessors, like the argument of those...
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage deals with the internal dispute among philosophers about the classification of the Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud). The author points out that the ancients, especially Aristotle, did not treat the First Cause as falling under the category of substance. Aristotle spoke of the 'First Cause' or 'Principle' (al-'illa al-ula, al-mabda'), not of a 'Necessary Being'. Ibn Sina (Avicenna) also refrained from classifying it as a substance. The author then makes a historical observation: the division of being into necessary and possible—which became common among Muslim Peripatetics like Ibn Sina—is not found in Aristotle. For Aristotle, the 'possible' (mumkin) that can be or not be is only originated (hadith), not eternal. This necessary/possible division was introduced by later philosophers whom the author labels as 'mulahida' (atheists) who pretended to be Muslims, such as Ibn Sina and his followers. They innovated this classification as a replacement for the Islamic theological division into eternal (qadim) and originated (hadith). The author criticizes their method as a hybrid (rakkabuha) of the theologians' discourse and that of their pagan predecessors. The passage ends with a reference to an example of their argument, though the page cuts off.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
الجوهر (مقولة)
العلة الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They used Aristotle's original division into substance and accidents.
B) They replaced the theological division (qadim/hadith) with necessary/possible.
C) They denied the category of substance entirely.
D) They agreed with the theologians.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows that the philosophical categories used in Mantiq are not universally accepted; they are historically contingent and subject to theological scrutiny.

Why the Intellectual World Is Not the Unseen World
Author refutes the identification of the unseen world with the intellectual world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's objection to the philosophers' equation of the unseen world with the intellectual world.

Core Idea
The philosophers' 'intellectuals' exist only in the mind—they are not the real unseen world.

Ibn Taymiyya confronts a central claim of the philosophers: that the unseen world (ālam al-ghayb) is identical to their intellectual world. In this passage, he exposes the error by clarifying that what they call 'intellectuals' have no existence outside the mind. He then explains their terminology of 'abstracts' and 'separates' to show that these are mental constructions, not realities of the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states the claim being refuted: that the unseen world is the intellectual world.
2
Analyze the refutation
Ibn Taymiyya says 'when investigated, they have no existence except in the mind'. How does he support this?
3
Understand the terminology
What do 'abstracts' (mujarradāt) and 'separates' (mufāraqāt) mean? Note the explanation: the mind abstracts universals from particulars.
4
Trace the origin of 'separates'
The term comes from the rational soul separating from the body. This is a key difference: the philosophers' separate entities are mental, not real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقام الرابع - الوجه الأول : قول من زعم أن عالم الغيب هو العالم العقلي وليس الأمر كذلك. فإن ما يثبتونه من «العقليات» إذا حقق الأمر لم يكن لها وجود إلا في العقل. وسُمّيت «مجردات» و «مفارقات»، لأن العقل يجرد الأمور الكلية عن المعينات. وأما تسميتها مفارقات فكان أصله أن النفس الناطقة تفارق البدن، وتصير حينئذ «عقلاً». وكانوا يسمون ما جامَعَ المادة بالتدبير لها كالنفس قبل الموت «نفس»، وما فارقها بالكلية فلم يتعلق بها لا تعلق تدبير ولا غيره «عقلاً». ولا ريب أن «النفس الناطقة قائمة بنفسها باقية بعد الموت منعَّمة أو معذَّبة، كما دلّ على ذلك نصوص الكتاب والسنة وإجماع سلف الأمة وأئمتها ؛ ثم تُعاد إلى الأبدان. ومن قال من أهل الكلام: إن النفس عَرَض من أعراض البدن أو جزء من أجزائه» فقوله بدعة، ولم يقل ذلك أحد من سلف الأمة .
The fourth station - the first aspect: The saying of one who claims that the unseen world is the intellectual world, but the matter is not so. For what they affirm of 'intellectuals' (al-ʻaqliyyāt), when the matter is investigated, has no existence except in the mind. They are called 'abstracts' (mujarradāt) and 'separates' (mufāraqāt), because the mind abstracts universal matters from particulars. As for naming them 'separates', its origin is that the rational soul separates from the body and then becomes 'intellect'. They used to name that which is conjoined with matter by managing it, like the soul before death, 'soul', and that which is completely separate and is not attached to it by any attachment of management or otherwise, 'intellect'. There is no doubt that the rational soul is self-subsisting, remaining after death, blissful or tormented, as indicated by the texts of the Book and the Sunnah and the consensus of the Salaf of the ummah and its imams; then it is returned to bodies. Whoever among the people of kalām says that the soul is an accident of the body's accidents or a part of its parts, his statement is an innovation, and none of the Salaf of the ummah said that.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the philosophers' claim that the unseen world (ʻālam al-ghayb) is identical to their intellectual world (al-ʻālam al-ʻaqlī). He states outright that this is not the case. He then explains that what the philosophers affirm as 'intellectuals' (al-ʻaqliyyāt) — entities known only through reason — have no existence outside the mind. These are called 'abstracts' (mujarradāt) because the mind abstracts universal concepts from particular objects. They are also called 'separates' (mufāraqāt), a term derived from the rational soul's separation from the body. The philosophers described that which manages the body as 'soul', and that which is completely detached from matter as 'intellect'. Ibn Taymiyya then emphasizes, using evidence from revelation and consensus, that the rational soul is self-subsisting, survives bodily death, and will be returned to the body on resurrection. He strongly condemns anyone from the kalām tradition who claims the soul is merely an accident or a part of the body as a heretic, affirming that this view was never held by the early righteous ancestors.

Key Terms
عالم الغيب
العقليات
مجردات
مفارقات
النفس الناطقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) In the external world as separate entities.
B) Only in the mind.
C) In the unseen world.
D) As accidents of the body.

Key Takeaway
The unseen world of revelation cannot be reduced to the philosophers' mental abstractions.

The Rational Soul: A Self-Subsisting Reality that Survives Death
Author affirms the rational soul's independent existence and refutes the accident theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to cite Ibn Taymiyya's evidence for the soul's independent existence and refute the view that it is an accident.

Core Idea
The rational soul survives death, blissful or tormented, as proven by Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

Having distinguished mental constructs from reality, Ibn Taymiyya now affirms a true unseen entity: the rational soul. He declares with certainty that the soul is self-subsisting and remains after death, being rewarded or punished, as attested by revelation and the consensus of the early Muslim community. He strongly rejects the kalām view that the soul is an accident or a part of the body.

Guided Path
1
Locate the key affirmation
Find where Ibn Taymiyya states that the rational soul is 'self-subsisting, remaining after death, blissful or tormented'.
2
Identify the evidence
What three sources does he cite? (Quran, Sunnah, consensus of the Salaf and imams).
3
Note the rejected view
What does Ibn Taymiyya call the opinion that the soul is an accident or part of the body? (It is an innovation, not said by any Salaf).
4
Connect to the previous lesson
How does this affirmation contrast with the philosophers' view that the soul becomes 'intellect' after death? Ibn Taymiyya affirms a concrete afterlife.

Critique of Abstract Essences
Refutation of abstract essences and their claim to external reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's objection to abstract essences and explain why he considers them mental constructs.

Core Idea
Abstract essences are mental fictions, not external realities.

Ibn Taymiyya targets a fundamental assumption of the logicians: that we can reason about essences stripped of all conditions, such as 'man in himself'. He argues that these are mere mental estimates, not truths about the external world, and compares this error to that of earlier philosophers regarding Platonic forms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' claim
Find where Ibn Taymiyya describes what the logicians do: they speak about abstract essences like 'man in itself', thinking these are real.
Look for phrases like 'يظنون أن هذه الماهية محققة في الخارج'.
2
Find Ibn Taymiyya's comparison
He compares this error to the error of earlier philosophers about numbers and Platonic forms. Identify the phrase 'غلط كغلط أوائهم'.
3
Locate his main argument
He states that these abstractions only exist as mental estimations. Look for 'إلا مقدرة في الذهن'.
This is the core of his critique.
4
Understand the refutation
He argues that supposing an essence abstracted from external and mental existence is itself a mental act, like supposing impossibilities.
The refutation is that mental abstraction does not grant external reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هنا يغلط كثير ممن يسلك سبيلهم حيث يظن أن ما عنده من القضايا الكلية صحيح، ويكون عند التحقيق ليس كذلك. وهم يتصورون الشيء بعقولهم، ويكون ما تصوروه معقولاً بالعقل، فيتكلمون عليه ويظنون أنهم تكلموا في ماهية مجردة بنفسها من حيث هي هي من غير أن تكون ثابتة في الخارج ولا في الذهن، فيقولون «الإنسان من حيث هو هو» و «الوجود من حيث هو هو» و «السواد من حيث هو هو»، ونحو ذلك. ويظنون أن هذه الماهية التي جردوها عن جميع القيود السلبية والثبوتية محققة في الخارج على هذا التجريد. وذلك غلط كغلط أوائهم فيما جردوه من «العدد» و «المثل الأفلاطونية» وغيرها. بل هذه المجردات المسلوب عنها كل قيد ثبوتي وسلبي لا تكون إلا مقدرة في الذهن. وإذا قال القائل : فأنا أفرض الإنسان مجرداً عن الوجودين الخارجي والذهني، قيل له : هذا الفرض في الذهن أيضاً كما تفرض سائر الممتنعات في الذهن، مثل أن يفرض موجوداً، لا واجباً ولا ممكناً، ولا قائماً بنفسه ولا بغيره، ولا مبايناً لغيره ولا مجانباً له؛ وهذا كله مفروض في الذهن وليس كل ما فرضه الذهن أمكن وجوده في الخارج وليس كل ما حكم به الإنسان على ما يقدره ويفرضه في ذهنه يكون حكماً صحيحاً على ما يوجد في الخارج؛ ولا كل ما أمكن تصور الذهن له يكون وجوده في الخارج. بل الذهن يتصور أشياء ويقدرها مع علمه بامتناعها، ومع علمه بإمكانها في الإمكان الخارج، ومع عدم علمه بالامتناع الخارجي والإمكان الخارجي. وهذا الذي يسمى الإمكان الذهني. فإن «الإمكان» يستعمل على وجهين: إمكان ذهني، وإمكان خارجي. فالإمكان الذهني أن يعرض الشيء على الذهن فلا يعلم امتناعه، بل يقول:
And from here many who follow their path err, where they suppose that what they have of universal propositions is correct, but upon investigation it is not so. And they conceive things with their minds, and what they conceive is intellectually grasped, so they speak about it and think they have spoken about an essence abstracted by itself, in terms of itself, without it being established in externality or in the mind. They say: 'Man in terms of himself', 'Existence in terms of itself', 'Blackness in terms of itself', and the like. And they suppose that this essence which they have stripped of all negative and positive conditions is actualized in externality in this stripping. That is an error, like the error of their predecessors in what they stripped from 'number' and the 'Platonic forms' and others. Rather, these abstractions, stripped of every positive and negative condition, only exist as estimated in the mind. And if someone says: 'I suppose man abstracted from external and mental existence,' it is said to him: This supposition is also in the mind, just as all impossibilities are supposed in the mind, such as supposing something existent that is neither necessary nor possible, neither subsistent by itself nor by another, neither distinct from another nor adjacent to it; all of this is supposed in the mind. And not everything the mind supposes is possible in existence externally, and not every judgment a person makes about what he estimates and supposes in his mind is a correct judgment about what exists externally; nor is everything whose conception is possible for the mind such that its existence is possible externally. Rather, the mind conceives things and estimates them while knowing their impossibility, while knowing their possibility in external possibility, and while not knowing external impossibility and external possibility. And this is called mental possibility. For 'possibility' is used in two ways: mental possibility and external possibility. Mental possibility is that a thing is presented to the mind and it does not know its impossibility, but rather says:
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a critique by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' habit of reasoning about abstract essences (mahiyat mujarrada) as if they have real external existence. He begins by stating that those who follow the logicians' path often err, thinking their universal propositions are correct when they are not. They conceive things mentally and then speak about a 'man in itself' or 'existence in itself,' believing these abstractions stripped of all conditions are actual in external reality. Ibn Taymiyya compares this to the error of earlier philosophers regarding numbers and Platonic forms. He asserts that these abstractions exist only as mental estimations. If someone claims to suppose 'man' abstracted from both external and mental existence, that supposition itself occurs only in the mind, like supposing impossibilities. Not every mental supposition implies external possibility, nor are mental judgments valid for external reality. He distinguishes between two types of possibility: mental possibility (when the mind does not know impossibility) and external possibility (actual possibility in the world). The text cuts off while defining mental possibility, but the argument is clear: mental abstraction does not grant external reality.

Key Terms
ماهية مجردة
مثل أفلاطونية

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It exists only as an estimation in the mind.
C) It is impossible to conceive.
D) It is a Platonic form.

Key Takeaway
Abstract essences are mental constructs, not external realities.

Mental vs External Possibility
Distinction between mental and external possibility and the error of conflating them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mental possibility and external possibility as defined by Ibn Taymiyya, and identify the error of conflating them.

Core Idea
Mental possibility is not evidence for external possibility.

Ibn Taymiyya continues his critique by clarifying the concept of possibility. He distinguishes between mental possibility (the mind does not perceive impossibility) and external possibility (actual feasibility in the world). Confusing the two leads to erroneous conclusions about what can exist.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two types of possibility
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly states that 'possibility' is used in two ways: mental and external. Identify where he lists them.
Look for 'فإن «الإمكان» يستعمل على وجهين'.
2
Define mental possibility
He defines mental possibility as when a thing is presented to the mind and the mind does not know its impossibility. Understand that this is a psychological state, not a property of the thing itself.
The text cuts off, but the beginning of the definition is clear: 'الإمكان الذهني أن يعرض الشيء على الذهن فلا يعلم امتناعه'.
3
Define external possibility
From context, external possibility is when a thing is not impossible in the external world. Contrast with mental possibility.
He implies that external possibility depends on actual existence conditions, not just mental conception.
4
Identify the error of conflation
Ibn Taymiyya warns that not everything the mind conceives or judges is possible externally. The error is to assume that mental possibility entails external possibility.
He says: 'ولا كل ما حكم به الإنسان على ما يقدره ويفرضه في ذهنه يكون حكماً صحيحاً على ما يوجد في الخارج'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هنا يغلط كثير ممن يسلك سبيلهم حيث يظن أن ما عنده من القضايا الكلية صحيح، ويكون عند التحقيق ليس كذلك. وهم يتصورون الشيء بعقولهم، ويكون ما تصوروه معقولاً بالعقل، فيتكلمون عليه ويظنون أنهم تكلموا في ماهية مجردة بنفسها من حيث هي هي من غير أن تكون ثابتة في الخارج ولا في الذهن، فيقولون «الإنسان من حيث هو هو» و «الوجود من حيث هو هو» و «السواد من حيث هو هو»، ونحو ذلك. ويظنون أن هذه الماهية التي جردوها عن جميع القيود السلبية والثبوتية محققة في الخارج على هذا التجريد. وذلك غلط كغلط أوائهم فيما جردوه من «العدد» و «المثل الأفلاطونية» وغيرها. بل هذه المجردات المسلوب عنها كل قيد ثبوتي وسلبي لا تكون إلا مقدرة في الذهن. وإذا قال القائل : فأنا أفرض الإنسان مجرداً عن الوجودين الخارجي والذهني، قيل له : هذا الفرض في الذهن أيضاً كما تفرض سائر الممتنعات في الذهن، مثل أن يفرض موجوداً، لا واجباً ولا ممكناً، ولا قائماً بنفسه ولا بغيره، ولا مبايناً لغيره ولا مجانباً له؛ وهذا كله مفروض في الذهن وليس كل ما فرضه الذهن أمكن وجوده في الخارج وليس كل ما حكم به الإنسان على ما يقدره ويفرضه في ذهنه يكون حكماً صحيحاً على ما يوجد في الخارج؛ ولا كل ما أمكن تصور الذهن له يكون وجوده في الخارج. بل الذهن يتصور أشياء ويقدرها مع علمه بامتناعها، ومع علمه بإمكانها في الإمكان الخارج، ومع عدم علمه بالامتناع الخارجي والإمكان الخارجي. وهذا الذي يسمى الإمكان الذهني. فإن «الإمكان» يستعمل على وجهين: إمكان ذهني، وإمكان خارجي. فالإمكان الذهني أن يعرض الشيء على الذهن فلا يعلم امتناعه، بل يقول:
And from here many who follow their path err, where they suppose that what they have of universal propositions is correct, but upon investigation it is not so. And they conceive things with their minds, and what they conceive is intellectually grasped, so they speak about it and think they have spoken about an essence abstracted by itself, in terms of itself, without it being established in externality or in the mind. They say: 'Man in terms of himself', 'Existence in terms of itself', 'Blackness in terms of itself', and the like. And they suppose that this essence which they have stripped of all negative and positive conditions is actualized in externality in this stripping. That is an error, like the error of their predecessors in what they stripped from 'number' and the 'Platonic forms' and others. Rather, these abstractions, stripped of every positive and negative condition, only exist as estimated in the mind. And if someone says: 'I suppose man abstracted from external and mental existence,' it is said to him: This supposition is also in the mind, just as all impossibilities are supposed in the mind, such as supposing something existent that is neither necessary nor possible, neither subsistent by itself nor by another, neither distinct from another nor adjacent to it; all of this is supposed in the mind. And not everything the mind supposes is possible in existence externally, and not every judgment a person makes about what he estimates and supposes in his mind is a correct judgment about what exists externally; nor is everything whose conception is possible for the mind such that its existence is possible externally. Rather, the mind conceives things and estimates them while knowing their impossibility, while knowing their possibility in external possibility, and while not knowing external impossibility and external possibility. And this is called mental possibility. For 'possibility' is used in two ways: mental possibility and external possibility. Mental possibility is that a thing is presented to the mind and it does not know its impossibility, but rather says:
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a critique by Ibn Taymiyya of the logicians' habit of reasoning about abstract essences (mahiyat mujarrada) as if they have real external existence. He begins by stating that those who follow the logicians' path often err, thinking their universal propositions are correct when they are not. They conceive things mentally and then speak about a 'man in itself' or 'existence in itself,' believing these abstractions stripped of all conditions are actual in external reality. Ibn Taymiyya compares this to the error of earlier philosophers regarding numbers and Platonic forms. He asserts that these abstractions exist only as mental estimations. If someone claims to suppose 'man' abstracted from both external and mental existence, that supposition itself occurs only in the mind, like supposing impossibilities. Not every mental supposition implies external possibility, nor are mental judgments valid for external reality. He distinguishes between two types of possibility: mental possibility (when the mind does not know impossibility) and external possibility (actual possibility in the world). The text cuts off while defining mental possibility, but the argument is clear: mental abstraction does not grant external reality.

Key Terms
الإمكان الذهني
الإمكان الخارجي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what is mental possibility?
A) A thing is known to be possible in the external world.
B) The mind conceives the thing as actually existing.
C) The mind does not know of the thing's impossibility.
D) The thing is impossible in the external world.

Key Takeaway
Mental possibility is ignorance of impossibility, not a mark of external possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
A theologian argues: 'Since we can conceive of an infinite chain of events, it must be externally possible.' Using Ibn Taymiyya's distinction, explain why this argument fails.

Identify a statement in a kalam text that uses mental possibility as evidence for external possibility. Apply Ibn Taymiyya's critique to assess its validity.

What Is Mantiq?

Defining External Possibility: Ibn Taymiyya's Empirical Alternative
Ibn Taymiyya defines external possibility and its three epistemic foundations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish Ibn Taymiyya's empirical definition of external possibility from the logicians' abstract notion and apply it to evaluate a theological claim.

Core Idea
External possibility is known from actual existence, not from lack of known impossibility.

Ibn Taymiyya opens this section by sharply criticizing the logicians' definition of possibility as mere non-contradiction. He proposes an alternative rooted in empirical reality: external possibility is knowledge that something exists in the external world or that something similar or even more remote exists. This redefinition lays the groundwork for his Quranic proofs of resurrection, turning abstract modal logic into a concrete evidentiary method. In this lesson, we will dissect his definition and understand its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logicians' view on possibility
Find where Ibn Taymiyya describes how logicians define possibility: 'this is possible, not because of his knowledge of its possibility but because of his lack of knowledge of its impossibility.' This is a purely epistemic (and negative) definition.
2
Locate Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He contrasts this with 'impossibility in external existence' and then defines 'external possibility' as knowledge of actual existence. Note the shift from epistemic to empirical.
3
Extract the three ways to know external possibility
The passage states: knowing the existence of the thing itself, or the existence of its like, or the existence of something further from existence. Write these down as they are the basis for the Quranic proofs.
The third way uses a 'fortiori reasoning: if the more distant exists, the closer is more deserving.
4
Apply the definition to a simple example
Suppose someone doubts whether a phoenix can exist. According to Ibn Taymiyya, we cannot claim it is externally possible without evidence of actual existence, a similar bird, or an even more improbable creature. This shows the practical consequence.
Islamic examples from the passage include historical resurrections, which are actual instances.
